\]

is the one-slot substitution marginal.

Equivalently, for every polynomial

\[
P(z)=a_1z+\cdots+a_dz^d
\]

with no constant term, prove

\[
\int |1-P(r(x))|^2\,d\mu(x)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The obstruction would be that \(r(x)\) is almost constant on the support of \(\mu\), or that \(\mu\) concentrates on at most \(d\) effective \(r\)-values.

Thus the exact needed property is:

\[
\boxed{
r_\#\mu
\text{ is not concentrated on fewer than }d+1\text{ polynomially separated atoms.}
}
\]

Christoffel spread is one way to prove this.

---

## 2. Christoffel spread alone is upper spread, not lower spread

The estimate

\[
\mu(J)\le R^C|J|
\]

is an upper bound on concentration. It prevents all mass from lying in a tiny interval, but it does **not** by itself guarantee that there are \(d+1\) separated intervals each with polynomial mass.

For example, \(\mu\) could be spread over one interval of length \(R^{-C}\), which respects the upper bound but still makes \(r\) nearly constant if that interval is away from regions of large derivative.

So we also need a lower support/mass condition:

\[
\boxed{
\mu(\Omega_{\mathrm{active}})\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

on an interval or union of intervals where \(r\) has enough variation.

More concretely, we need to find intervals

\[
J_0,\ldots,J_d
\]

such that:

\[
\mu(J_i)\ge Q^{-A},
\]

and

\[
\operatorname{dist}(r(J_i),r(J_j))\ge Q^{-A}
\quad (i\ne j).
\]

Then the Vandermonde lower bound works.

So the O2/O3 near-pure theorem needs both:

\[
\boxed{\text{Christoffel upper spread}}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{\text{active support lower spread}}.
\]

---

## 3. How to obtain lower spread

A natural condition is that \(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}\) has polynomial mass on an interval

\[
\Omega_*\subset [c_-,c_+]
\]

of polynomial length:

\[
|\Omega_*|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
\mu(\Omega_*)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

If \(r\) is monotone on \(\Omega_*\) and

\[
|r'(x)|\ge Q^{-A}
\quad
\text{on }\Omega_*,
\]

then \(r(\Omega_*)\) has length at least

\[
Q^{-A}.
\]

Since \(d=O(1)\), choose \(d+1\) disjoint subintervals \(J_i\subset \Omega_*\) with

\[
|J_i|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

and \(r(J_i)\) separated by \(Q^{-A}\).

Christoffel upper spread gives only

\[
\mu(J_i)\le R^C|J_i|.
\]

For lower mass, we need either:

1. a lower Christoffel-type density bound,

\[
\mu(J_i)\ge Q^{-A}|J_i|
\]

on the selected interval; or

2. choose \(J_i\) by quantiles of \(\mu\), then use upper spread to show they have nontrivial length and hence \(r\)-separation.

The quantile route is better.

---

## 4. Quantile proof of separated \(r\)-values

Assume:

\[
\mu(\Omega_*)\ge Q^{-A_0},
\]

\[
\mu(J)\le R^{A_1}|J|
\quad
\text{for all intervals }J\subset\Omega_*,
\]

and

\[
|r'(x)|\ge Q^{-A_2}
\quad
\text{on }\Omega_*.
\]

Partition \(\Omega_*\) into \(d+1\) consecutive \(\mu\)-quantile intervals

\[
J_0,\ldots,J_d
\]

with

\[
\mu(J_i)\asymp \frac{\mu(\Omega_*)}{d+1}\ge Q^{-A_0-O(1)}.
\]

The upper spread gives

\[
|J_i|\ge R^{-A_1}\mu(J_i)\ge Q^{-A_3}.
\]

Since \(r'\) is bounded below,

\[
|r(J_i)|\ge Q^{-A_2}|J_i|\ge Q^{-A_4}.
\]

Between separated quantile intervals, select points \(x_i\in J_i\). Monotonicity gives

\[
|r(x_i)-r(x_j)|\ge Q^{-A_5}
\]

after possibly merging only a bounded number of adjacent intervals.

Because \(d=O(1)\), polynomial losses remain polynomial.

Thus the finite moment theorem follows.

This is more robust than assuming pre-existing fixed intervals.

---

## 5. Vandermonde lower bound

Let

\[
z_i=r(x_i),\qquad i=0,\ldots,d,
\]

with

\[
|z_i-z_j|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Suppose

\[
|1-P(z_i)|\le \epsilon
\]

for all \(i\), where

\[
\deg P\le d,\quad P(0)=0.
\]

Then

\[
Q(z)=1-P(z)
\]

is a degree-\(d\) polynomial with constant term \(1\).

The evaluation map

\[
Q\mapsto (Q(z_0),\ldots,Q(z_d))
\]

has inverse norm bounded by a power of the inverse Vandermonde:

\[
\|Q\|_{\mathrm{coeff}}
\le
Q^A
\max_i |Q(z_i)|.
\]

Since the constant coefficient of \(Q\) is \(1\),

\[
1\le Q^A\max_i |Q(z_i)|.
\]

Therefore

\[
\max_i|1-P(z_i)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Because each \(J_i\) carries mass \(\ge Q^{-A}\), and \(r\) varies only polynomially across a small subinterval chosen inside \(J_i\), we get

\[
\int |1-P(r(x))|^2d\mu(x)\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

So the finite moment lower bound is established.

---

## 6. Endpoint and central-tube exceptions

The proof needs an interval \(\Omega_*\) where \(r'\) is polynomially non-small and \(r\) is monotone.

For

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2},
\]

we have

\[
\frac{d}{dx}\log r(x)
=
-2\left(\frac1{1-x}+\frac1x\right).
\]

On any compact subinterval of \((0,1)\),

\[
|r'(x)|\ge c>0
\]

unless \(r\) itself becomes degenerate. In fact \(r\) is strictly monotone on \((0,1)\).

The central tube \(x\approx 1/2\) is not a derivative-zero problem; \(r'(1/2)=-8\). The central tube is dangerous because

\[
r(x)\approx1
\]

and plus/dual amplitudes match. But for fixed-degree moment separation, \(r\) still varies linearly:

\[
r(1/2+\delta y)
=
1-8\delta y+O(\delta^2y^2).
\]

If the mass is contained in a tube of width \(\delta\), then \(r\)-variation is \(\asymp \delta\). Taking

\[
\delta=R^{-C}
\]

gives

\[
\delta^d=R^{-O(1)}
\]

because \(d=O(1)\). This is even better than the exterior-degree \(\delta^R\) loss.

So for the near-pure \(d_0=O(1)\) Schur block, the central tube is less dangerous than previously feared. The hard central loss \(\delta^R\) belongs to full exterior graph non-polynomiality, not the fixed-degree ratio moment once the bounded-charge reduction is made.

This is a useful simplification.

---

## 7. Revised central-tube conclusion

For the bounded-charge near-pure problem, central concentration is acceptable provided the tube has polynomial width:

\[
\delta\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Since choosing

\[
\delta=R^{-C}=Q^{-o(1)}
\]

is much larger than any fixed negative power \(Q^{-A}\), the moment lower bound survives.

Therefore, in the fixed-degree Schur-complement reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{central tube routing is not a serious obstruction.}
}
\]

It matters more for the full degree-\(R\) graph approximation, but not for the final \(d_0=O(1)\) near-pure survival problem.

This makes the fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 route more plausible.

---

## 8. Remaining obstruction: lower mass of \(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}\)

The real condition is not central routing. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}\text{ must have polynomial mass on a polynomial-length active interval.}
}
\]

If the pure plus exterior determinant ensemble itself concentrates on a tiny \(x\)-set, the moment argument fails.

But a Riesz-normalized Vandermonde/polynomial ensemble should have broad support unless the underlying branch support is tiny.

So the required theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Substitution marginal lower support: }
\exists \Omega_*,\ |\Omega_*|\ge Q^{-A},\ \mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(\Omega_*)\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This should follow from:

1. pure plus determinant mass nontriviality;
2. active plus branch support not microscopic;
3. Riesz normalization / Christoffel bounds.

This is now the exact remaining finite O2/O3 support condition.

---

## 9. Updated theorem

The near-pure theorem can be stated as:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{NearPureMomentSeparation.}
}
\]

Let \(d=d_0=O(1)\). Let \(\mu\) be the one-slot substitution marginal. Suppose there exists an interval \(\Omega_*\subset(0,1)\) such that:

\[
\mu(\Omega_*)\ge Q^{-A},
\]

\[
\mu(J)\le Q^A|J|
\quad\text{for every interval }J\subset\Omega_*,
\]

and

\[
|r'(x)|\ge Q^{-A}
\quad\text{on }\Omega_*.
\]

Then

\[
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\mu)}
\left(
1,
\operatorname{span}\{r,r^2,\ldots,r^d\}
\right)^2
\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

For

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2},
\]

the derivative condition is automatic on polynomially separated subintervals of \((0,1)\).

---

## 10. Final conclusion of this attack

The fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 near-pure route is stronger than it initially looked.

Once \(Y12\) gives \(d_0=O(1)\), determinant survival reduces to a fixed-degree moment problem, and the central tube is manageable.

The real remaining input is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the substitution marginal } \mu_{\mathrm{sub}}
\text{ has polynomial lower support and Christoffel upper spread.}
}
\]

With that, the moment lower bound for

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}
\]

is essentially finite-dimensional linear algebra.

## Assistant (2026-04-28 05:31:16)

I’ll now attack the remaining finite O2/O3 support condition: prove that the one-slot substitution marginal cannot collapse to a tiny set if the pure-plus exterior determinant has polynomial mass.

_Thought for 9s_

Continuing Agent 5 — substitution marginal lower support for the near-pure O2/O3 route.

The previous reduction showed that fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 survival is now essentially finite-dimensional once \(d_0=O(1)\). The remaining input is:

\[
\boxed{
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}
\text{ has polynomial lower support and Christoffel upper spread.}
}
\]

Here \(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}\) is the one-slot substitution marginal of the pure-plus exterior determinant ensemble, and the key ratio is

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}.
\]

This fits the current architecture: use smooth bounded-degree weights first, prove branch survival, then cap/extract only afterward. 

## 1. Pure-plus determinant mass defines the substitution ensemble

Let the pure-plus determinant component be

\[
D_{-n}
=
\sum_{S:\ |S|=n}
C_S e^{-im\log N_S},
\qquad n=R+2.
\]

The exterior coefficient mass is

\[
\mathcal M_+
=
\sum_{S}|C_S|^2.
\]

A natural one-slot marginal is

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(A)
=
\frac{1}{n\mathcal M_+}
\sum_{S}|C_S|^2
\#\{x\in S:\ x\in A\}.
\]

Thus \(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}\) records where a randomly selected slot of a randomly selected pure-plus exterior term lies.

The support condition we need is:

\[
\exists \Omega_*\subset(0,1)
\quad\text{such that}\quad
|\Omega_*|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(\Omega_*)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

If this fails, then the pure-plus determinant mass is generated by slots concentrated in a microscopic region. That would be a strong structural degeneracy of the exterior determinant.

---

## 2. Christoffel upper spread

In an \(R^C\)-Riesz normalized polynomial/exterior basis, the one-slot marginal has the form

\[
d\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(x)
=
\frac{1}{n}K_n(x,x)w(x)\,dx,
\]

where

\[
K_n(x,x)=\sum_{j=0}^{n-1}|p_j(x)|^2.
\]

The desired upper spread estimate is

\[
\boxed{
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(J)\le R^C |J|
}
\]

for intervals \(J\) in the active branch support.

This is standard from Christoffel kernel control once the basis is Riesz-normalized and the weight \(w\) is not endpoint-singular at super-polynomial scale.

So upper spread is plausible if the O2/O3 \(R^C\)-normalization audit succeeds.

---

## 3. Lower support follows from active branch support

Upper spread alone does not force lower support. But if the pure-plus determinant has polynomial mass and the active plus branch is not itself microscopic, then lower support follows.

Let \(\Omega_+\) be the active plus branch interval. Assume:

\[
|\Omega_+|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

and the pure-plus mass is genuinely supported there:

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(\Omega_+)\ge 1-Q^{-A}.
\]

Then choose

\[
\Omega_*=\Omega_+
\]

or a subinterval avoiding endpoints and singular points. We get the needed lower support.

Thus the real condition is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the pure-plus exterior determinant is not supported on a microscopic plus-frequency set.}
}
\]

This is a support/noncollapse property of the pure-plus determinant itself.

---

## 4. Why microscopic support is unlikely under Riesz normalization

Suppose, for contradiction, that

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(\Omega_*)<Q^{-A}
\]

for every interval \(\Omega_*\) of polynomial length. Then almost all exterior mass sits in a union of intervals of total length \(Q^{-A}\) or smaller.

But the exterior determinant includes a Vandermonde factor:

\[
(\det V_S)^2.
\]

If all slots \(x_i\) lie inside an interval of length \(\delta\), then

\[
|\det V_S|^2
\]

is suppressed by approximately

\[
\prod_{i<j}|x_i-x_j|^2
\le
\delta^{n(n-1)}.
\]

With

\[
n=R+2,
\]

and \(\delta=Q^{-A}\), this is

\[
Q^{-A n(n-1)}
=
\exp\!\left(-A R^2\log Q\right),
\]

which is far smaller than any allowed polynomial scale.

Thus any exterior component carrying polynomial determinant mass must use slots separated over a polynomial-length region. This gives a stronger mechanism than Christoffel spread.

The finite theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Vandermonde mass noncollapse}
\Longrightarrow
\text{substitution marginal lower support}.
}
\]

---

## 5. Quantitative lower-support lemma

A usable lemma:

Assume the exterior coefficient mass satisfies

\[
\mathcal M_+\ge Q^{-A}\mathcal M_{\mathrm{ref}},
\]

where \(\mathcal M_{\mathrm{ref}}\) is the natural branch reference mass. Assume also that all coefficients outside a branch interval \(\Omega_+\) are negligible.

If for every interval \(J\) of length \(\delta\),

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(J)>1-Q^{-A},
\]

then most \(n\)-tuples contributing to \(\mathcal M_+\) have all slots in \(J\). Their Vandermonde factor loses

\[
\delta^{n(n-1)}.
\]

Taking

\[
\delta=R^{-C}
\]

already gives

\[
\delta^{n(n-1)}
=
R^{-C R^2}
=
\exp(-C R^2\log R),
\]

which is super-polynomially small in the \(R\)-scale and too small to support the polynomial determinant mass.

Therefore there must exist a polynomial-length interval \(\Omega_*\) with polynomial marginal mass outside any microscopic concentration. In particular, there are at least \(d_0+1\) separated regions with polynomial marginal mass.

This is exactly what the finite moment argument needs.

---

## 6. The central tube revisited

If \(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}\) concentrates near

\[
x=\frac12
\]

inside a tube of width

\[
\delta=R^{-C},
\]

then the Vandermonde penalty is not automatically fatal if the tube still has width \(R^{-C}\), because

\[
\delta^{R}
=
Q^{-O(1)}
\]

for fixed-degree moment losses, though \(\delta^{R^2}\) may be severe for full exterior determinant mass.

For the near-pure moment theorem, central concentration at scale \(R^{-C}\) is acceptable. The ratio

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}
\]

varies by \(R^{-C}\), and because \(d_0=O(1)\), the Vandermonde lower bound in \(r\)-space loses only

\[
R^{-O(1)}.
\]

So the problematic case is not concentration in an \(R^{-C}\)-scale central tube. It is concentration at a super-polynomially smaller scale, or concentration on too few effective \(r\)-values.

Thus the support theorem should use \(R\)-polynomial scales, not \(Q\)-polynomial scales:

\[
\boxed{
\text{need support at scale }R^{-C},\text{ not necessarily }Q^{-C}.
}
\]

This is important because \(R^{-C}\) losses remain \(Q^{-o(1)}\).

---

## 7. Final near-pure support package

The fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 route now needs the following finite package.

### Pure-plus exterior mass

\[
\int |D_{-n}|^2 W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge Q^{-A}\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
\]

This is supplied by the phase-aligned/graph non-polynomiality theorem.

### Riesz/Christoffel upper spread

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(J)\le R^C |J|.
\]

### Vandermonde noncollapse / lower support

There exist \(d_0+1\) intervals

\[
J_0,\ldots,J_{d_0}
\]

such that

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(J_i)\ge R^{-C},
\]

and

\[
\operatorname{dist}(r(J_i),r(J_j))\ge R^{-C}.
\]

Then finite-dimensional polynomial interpolation gives

\[
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}})}
\left(
1,\operatorname{span}\{r,\ldots,r^{d_0}\}
\right)^2
\ge R^{-C}
=
Q^{-o(1)}.
\]

This is more than enough.

---

## 8. Current conclusion

The substitution marginal support condition is not a new deep arithmetic theorem if the pure-plus determinant has polynomial exterior mass in an \(R^C\)-Riesz basis.

It follows from:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Christoffel upper spread}
+
\text{Vandermonde mass noncollapse}.
}
\]

Therefore fixed-\(\alpha\) near-pure O2/O3 survival is now reduced to two concrete finite audits:

\[
\boxed{
\|A_m\|\le R^C
\text{ in a Riesz basis}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
D_{-n}
\text{ has polynomial mass before branch mixing}.
}
\]

Once those hold, the near-pure Schur complement follows by finite-dimensional interpolation for

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}.
\]

This strengthens the fixed-\(\alpha\) route and reduces reliance on \(\alpha\)-averaging.

## Assistant (2026-04-28 05:33:34)

I’ll now push the remaining O2/O3 input: polynomial mass of the pure-plus exterior determinant \(D_{-n}\). If that follows from the graph distance \(\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])\), then the fixed-\(\alpha\) route becomes substantially cleaner.

_Thought for 6s_

Continuing Agent 5 — pure-plus determinant mass from the graph non-polynomiality of \(1/m_{\can,+}\).

The previous attack reduced fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 survival to:

\[
D_{-n}
\notin
\operatorname{span}\{D_{-n+1},\ldots,D_{-n+d_0}\},
\]

and then to finite moment separation for

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}.
\]

That reduction assumes the pure-plus determinant \(D_{-n}\) has polynomial mass before branch mixing. I’ll now attack that input.

## 1. Pure-plus determinant is the graph-volume of \(1/m_{\can,+}\)

The pure-plus augmented space is

\[
\mathbf C\cdot 1 + m_{\can,+}\mathbf C_R[x].
\]

Factor out \(m_{\can,+}\):

\[
\mathbf C\cdot 1 + m_{\can,+}\mathbf C_R[x]
=
m_{\can,+}\left(\mathbf C\cdot f+\mathbf C_R[x]\right),
\qquad
f=\frac{1}{m_{\can,+}}.
\]

Thus the pure-plus determinant volume is controlled by the distance of \(f\) from \(\mathbf C_R[x]\).

In a Riesz-normalized basis,

\[
|D_{-n}|^2
\asymp
\operatorname{Vol}\!\left(\mathbf C f+\mathbf C_R[x]\right)^2
\cdot
\operatorname{Jac}(m_{\can,+})^2.
\]

More concretely,

\[
\operatorname{Vol}\!\left(\mathbf C f+\mathbf C_R[x]\right)^2
=
\operatorname{dist}(f,\mathbf C_R[x])^2
\cdot
\operatorname{Vol}(\mathbf C_R[x])^2.
\]

If the polynomial block is Riesz-normalized so that

\[
\operatorname{Vol}(\mathbf C_R[x])^2\asymp 1,
\]

then

\[
\boxed{
|D_{-n}|^2
\gtrsim
Q^{-A}
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])^2
}
\]

up to multiplier Jacobian factors.

So the pure-plus determinant lower mass follows from:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is exactly the graph/O2O3 analytic theorem.

---

## 2. Model calculation: \(m_{\can,+}(x)\sim \kappa x^2\)

If

\[
m_{\can,+}(x)=\kappa x^2(1+\varepsilon(x)),
\]

then

\[
f(x)=\frac1{m_{\can,+}(x)}
=
\frac1{\kappa x^2}(1+O(\varepsilon(x))).
\]

The model obstruction is:

\[
\operatorname{dist}(x^{-2},\mathbf C_R[x]).
\]

On an interval avoiding \(x=0\), this distance could be very small if \(x^{-2}\) is analytic on a large Bernstein ellipse around the interval. But in the O2/O3 setup the relevant branch interval is tied to the singularity/curvature scale. The non-polynomiality is useful only if the pole at \(x=0\) is close enough to the active interval.

A finite Remez/Chebyshev estimate gives roughly:

\[
\operatorname{dist}(x^{-2},\mathbf C_R[x])
\gtrsim
\rho^{-R},
\]

where \(\rho>1\) is the Bernstein ellipse parameter determined by the distance from the active interval to the pole.

We need

\[
\rho^{-R}\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Since

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

this requires

\[
\log\rho=O(\log\log Q).
\]

That is easy if \(\rho=O(1)\), and still acceptable for many polynomially growing \(\rho\). The dangerous case is when the active interval is so far from the singularity that

\[
\rho^R
\]

becomes super-polynomial.

Therefore the pure-plus determinant mass requires the active branch to remain in a region where

\[
1/m_{\can,+}
\]

has only polynomial-degree approximability by \(\mathbf C_R[x]\).

This is the analytic non-polynomiality theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}=Q^{-O(1)}.
}
\]

---

## 3. Central/near-singular scaling helps, not hurts

If the active interval approaches the zero of \(m_{\can,+}\), write

\[
x=\delta y,
\]

with

\[
m_{\can,+}(x)\sim \kappa \delta^2 y^2.
\]

Then

\[
\frac1{m_{\can,+}(x)}
\sim
\delta^{-2}\frac1{\kappa y^2}.
\]

The scalar factor \(\delta^{-2}\) is harmless after normalization. The approximation problem becomes

\[
\operatorname{dist}(y^{-2},\mathbf C_R[y])
\]

on a fixed \(y\)-interval, which has \(e^{-O(R)}\) or \(e^{-O(R\log R)}\) lower bounds depending on the interval.

Thus central/singular rescaling does not kill pure-plus determinant mass. It preserves it with polynomial loss.

This mirrors the earlier central-tube conclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{rescale the central zone; do not discard it.}
}
\]

---

## 4. Perturbation by \(1+\varepsilon\)

Let

\[
f=f_0+\Delta f,
\qquad
f_0=\frac1{\kappa x^2}.
\]

If

\[
\|\Delta f\|_{L^2}
\le
\frac12
\operatorname{dist}(f_0,\mathbf C_R[x]),
\]

then

\[
\operatorname{dist}(f,\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge
\frac12
\operatorname{dist}(f_0,\mathbf C_R[x]).
\]

So the analytic multiplier audit must prove:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\frac1{m_{\can,+}}-\frac1{\kappa x^2}
\right\|_{\mathrm{quotient}}
\le
\frac12
\operatorname{dist}\!\left(\frac1{\kappa x^2},\mathbf C_R[x]\right).
}
\]

A derivative version is also useful:

\[
\left|
\left(
\frac1{m_{\can,+}}-\frac1{\kappa x^2}
\right)^{(R+1)}
\right|
\le
\eta
\left|
\left(\frac1{\kappa x^2}\right)^{(R+1)}
\right|.
\]

By Hermite interpolation, this implies the divided-difference lower mass used in determinant coefficients.

---

## 5. Riesz normalization converts graph distance to determinant mass

Suppose the plus branch polynomial block has an \(R^C\)-Riesz basis:

\[
\|P\|_{L^2}\asymp \|p\|_{\ell^2},
\qquad
\|m_{\can,+}P\|_{L^2}\asymp \|P\|_{L^2}
\]

with losses \(\le R^C\), after the correct branch normalization.

Then

\[
\|A_{+}\|\le R^C,
\]

and the pure-plus determinant satisfies

\[
|D_{-n}|^2
\ge
R^{-C R}
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])^2.
\]

Since

\[
R^{-CR}=Q^{-O(1)},
\]

we obtain polynomial determinant mass from polynomial graph distance.

Thus the theorem stack is:

\[
\boxed{
R^C\text{-Riesz normalization}
+
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}
\Longrightarrow
\int |D_{-n}|^2 W_{\mathrm{bd}}\ge Q^{-A}\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
}
\]

This is the missing pure-plus mass theorem.

---

## 6. Full determinant survival then follows by near-pure Schur

Once pure-plus mass holds, the previous reduction gives:

\[
D_{-n}
\notin
\operatorname{span}\{D_{-n+1},\ldots,D_{-n+d_0}\}.
\]

The Schur complement gives

\[
\operatorname{dist}^2(D_{-n},\text{near-pure span})
\ge Q^{-A}\|D_{-n}\|^2.
\]

Then branch-charge reduction gives

\[
\int |D_{\mathrm{full}}|^2W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge
Q^{-A}
\int |D_{-n}|^2W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
\]

Combining:

\[
\boxed{
\int |D_{\mathrm{full}}|^2W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge Q^{-A}\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
}
\]

And with \(R^C\)-normalization,

\[
\boxed{
\int \mathcal H_{23}(A_m)W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge Q^{-A}\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
}
\]

So O2/O3 is reduced to three finite analytic inputs:

1. \(R^C\)-Riesz normalization;
2. graph non-polynomiality of \(1/m_{\can,+}\);
3. near-pure moment separation using Christoffel spread.

---

## 7. The current O2/O3 proof skeleton

The fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 route can now be summarized:

\[
\boxed{
m_{\can,+}\sim \kappa x^2
}
\]

gives

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Riesz normalization gives

\[
\boxed{
\|D_{-n}\|_{L^2(W_{\mathrm{bd}})}^2\ge Q^{-A}\|W_{\mathrm{bd}}\|_1.
}
\]

Bounded branch charge gives interaction only with

\[
D_{-n+1},\ldots,D_{-n+d_0}.
\]

Christoffel spread and substitution-ratio monotonicity give

\[
\boxed{
D_{-n}
\notin
\operatorname{span}\{D_{-n+1},\ldots,D_{-n+d_0}\}.
}
\]

Therefore full determinant survival holds, and \(R^C\)-normalization gives aggregate \(\mathcal H_{23}\).

This is now a coherent O2/O3 finite path.

---

## 8. Remaining possible obstruction

The main risk is that the active branch interval is too far from the zero/pole structure of \(m_{\can,+}\). Then

\[
1/m_{\can,+}
\]

could be exponentially well approximated by degree \(R\) polynomials, making

\[
D_{-n}
\]

too small.

This must be excluded by branch routing:

\[
\boxed{
\text{ordinary plus branch used for O2/O3 must lie where }1/m_{\can,+}
\text{ has only polynomial degree-}R\text{ approximability.}
}
\]

If this fails, O2/O3 must be routed to a different branch/scale.

So the analytic audit is:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge Q^{-A}
\text{ on the active Stack C2 support.}
}
\]

This is the current core O2/O3 finite-symbol theorem.

---

## Final result of this attack

The pure-plus determinant mass is not an independent arithmetic mystery.

It follows from:

\[
\boxed{
\text{graph non-polynomiality of }1/m_{\can,+}
}
\]

plus

\[
\boxed{
R^C\text{-Riesz normalization}.
}
\]

Then fixed-\(\alpha\) full O2/O3 follows from the bounded-charge near-pure Schur complement.

This reduces O2/O3 to a largely finite analytic chain, with the remaining hard input being the graph-distance estimate for the actual canonical multiplier.

## 1. Pure-plus graph theorem

For the pure plus branch, the augmented space is

\[
\mathbf C\cdot 1+m_{\mathrm{can},+}\mathbf C_R[x].
\]

Factor:

\[
\mathbf C\cdot 1+m_{\mathrm{can},+}\mathbf C_R[x]
=
m_{\mathrm{can},+}
\left(
\mathbf C\cdot f+\mathbf C_R[x]
\right),
\qquad
f=\frac1{m_{\mathrm{can},+}}.
\]

The local pure-plus O2/O3 obstruction is exactly

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(f,\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

In the model case

\[
m_{\mathrm{can},+}(x)\sim \kappa x^2,
\]

this becomes

\[
\operatorname{dist}(x^{-2},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The finite analytic condition is that the active branch interval must stay close enough to the pole/curvature scale that degree-\(R\) polynomial approximation is only polynomially good:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\mathrm{can},+},\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}=Q^{-O(1)}.
}
\]

If the active branch lies too far from the singularity of \(1/m_{\mathrm{can},+}\), this distance can be super-polynomially small. That is the branch-routing condition.

---

## 2. Pure-plus determinant mass

Assume an \(R^C\)-conditioned analytic Riesz basis for the domain/codomain of the finite augmented matrix. Then

\[
\|A_{+}\|\le R^C,
\]

and the pure-plus determinant satisfies

\[
\boxed{
|D_{-n}|^2
\ge
R^{-CR}\,
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\mathrm{can},+},\mathbf C_R[x])^2
}
\]

relative to the normalized pure-plus exterior volume, where

\[
n=R+2.
\]

Because

\[
R^{-CR}=Q^{-O(1)},
\]

the graph theorem gives

\[
\boxed{
\int |D_{-n}|^2 W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge
Q^{-A}
\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
}
\]

So pure-plus determinant mass follows from:

\[
\boxed{
R^C\text{-Riesz normalization}
+
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\mathrm{can},+},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

## 3. Substitution marginal

Define the pure-plus determinant expansion

\[
D_{-n}
=
\sum_{|S|=n} C_S e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

The one-slot substitution marginal is

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(A)
=
\frac{1}{n\sum_S|C_S|^2}
\sum_S |C_S|^2
\#\{x_j\in S:x_j\in A\}.
\]

In an \(R^C\)-Riesz-normalized polynomial ensemble,

\[
d\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(x)
=
\frac1nK_n(x,x)w(x)\,dx,
\]

where

\[
K_n(x,x)=\sum_{j=0}^{n-1}|p_j(x)|^2.
\]

The required upper spread is

\[
\boxed{
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(J)\le R^C |J|
}
\]

for intervals \(J\) in the active support.

This is a standard Christoffel-kernel consequence of \(R^C\)-Riesz normalization.

---

## 4. Lower support from determinant mass

Upper spread is not enough. We need polynomial lower support:

\[
\boxed{
\exists \Omega_*,\quad |\Omega_*|\ge Q^{-A},\quad
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(\Omega_*)\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This follows from pure-plus determinant mass and Vandermonde noncollapse.

Reason: the exterior coefficients include

\[
(\det V_S)^2.
\]

If almost all substitution mass lived in an interval of microscopic length \(\delta\), then the Vandermonde factor would lose

\[
\delta^{n(n-1)}.
\]

For

\[
n=R+2,
\]

a super-polynomially small \(\delta\) would make the determinant mass too small. Therefore polynomial determinant mass forces the slots to occupy a polynomial-length active region.

At the \(R\)-scale, it is enough to prove existence of \(d_0+1\) intervals

\[
J_0,\ldots,J_{d_0}
\]

with

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(J_i)\ge R^{-C},
\]

and separated \(r\)-images. This is weaker and sufficient.

---

## 5. Finite moment separation

The near-pure sectors differ by substituting plus slots by dual/background/tail slots. The basic plus-to-dual ratio is

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}.
\]

For

\[
d=d_0=O(1),
\]

we need

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}})}
\left(
1,\operatorname{span}\{r,r^2,\ldots,r^d\}
\right)^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Given \(d+1\) intervals \(J_i\) with polynomial \(\mu\)-mass and separated \(r\)-values, this follows by a Vandermonde argument.

If

\[
P(z)=a_1z+\cdots+a_dz^d,
\]

then

\[
Q(z)=1-P(z)
\]

has degree \(d\) and constant coefficient \(1\). It cannot be \(Q^{-A}\)-small at \(d+1\) separated \(z_i\)-values because the inverse Vandermonde has only polynomial loss for polynomially separated points.

Thus

\[
\int |1-P(r(x))|^2\,d\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(x)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This proves the fixed-degree moment lower bound.

---

## 6. Multiple substitution ratios

If near-pure sectors include background/tail substitutions, we get finitely many ratios:

\[
R_1(x),\ldots,R_M(x).
\]

The needed theorem becomes

\[
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\mu_{\mathrm{sub}})}
\left(
1,
\operatorname{span}\{
R_1^{a_1}\cdots R_M^{a_M}:
1\le a_1+\cdots+a_M\le d_0
\}
\right)^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This still has fixed dimension because \(d_0=O(1)\).

The ratio \(r(x)=(1-x)^2/x^2\) suffices as a separating coordinate if each \(R_j\) is either perturbative or a nonconstant function of \(r\) with polynomially controlled distortion.

The obstruction would be an algebraic identity:

\[
1\in
\operatorname{span}\{
R_1^{a_1}\cdots R_M^{a_M}
\}.
\]

So the multiple-ratio extension requires a finite algebraic independence/non-lock condition.

---

## 7. Central tube

For the fixed-degree moment problem, the central tube is not a serious obstruction.

Near

\[
x=\frac12,
\]

write

\[
x=\frac12+\delta y.
\]

Then

\[
r(x)
=
1-8\delta y+O(\delta^2y^2).
\]

If

\[
\delta=R^{-C},
\]

then variation in \(r\) is only \(R^{-C}\), but because \(d_0=O(1)\), the Vandermonde loss is only

\[
R^{-O(1)}=Q^{-o(1)}.
\]

So central concentration at \(R^{-C}\)-scale is still compatible with the near-pure Schur complement.

The central tube is harder for the full degree-\(R\) graph problem, but after bounded-charge reduction to \(d_0=O(1)\), it is manageable.

Thus the fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 route does not require \(\alpha\)-averaging to handle the central fold.

---

## 8. Final theorem chain

The fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 theorem can be stated as:

### Theorem: BoundedChargeNearPureO2O3

Assume:

1. **Riesz normalization**

\[
\|A_m\|\le R^C.
\]

2. **Graph non-polynomiality**

\[
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\mathrm{can},+},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

3. **Pure-plus determinant mass**

follows from 1 and 2:

\[
\int |D_{-n}|^2W_{\mathrm{bd}}\ge Q^{-A}\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
\]

4. **Branch-charge boundedness**

\[
d_0=d_N+2d_H+2d_\Gamma=O(1).
\]

5. **Christoffel upper spread**

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}(J)\le R^C|J|.
\]

6. **Substitution lower support**

\[
\mu_{\mathrm{sub}}
\]

has \(d_0+1\) polynomially separated \(r\)-values carrying polynomial mass.

Then

\[
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
D_{-n},
\operatorname{span}\{D_{-n+1},\ldots,D_{-n+d_0}\}
\right)^2
\ge
Q^{-A}
\|D_{-n}\|^2.
\]

Therefore

\[
\int |D_{\mathrm{full}}|^2W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge
Q^{-A}
\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
\]

Finally, by \(R^C\)-normalization,

\[
\boxed{
\int \mathcal H_{23}(A_m)W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge
Q^{-A}
\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
}
\]

The theorem is now finite. The only unresolved inputs are:

All are substantially more concrete than the earlier determinant collision problem.

The fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 route is now viable in principle.

}

after using the \(Y12\)-based wall factor. This turns full determinant survival from a high-dimensional branch-collision problem into a fixed-degree moment separation problem for

_Thought for 7s_

Continuing Agent 5 — graph non-polynomiality for \(1/m_{\can,+}\).

The fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 chain now reduces to a finite analytic input:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

in an \(R^C\)-conditioned Riesz basis on the active plus branch. If this holds, pure-plus determinant mass follows, then Christoffel spread and finite moment separation give the near-pure Schur complement. 

## 1. Correct form of the theorem

The theorem should not be stated globally on every interval. It should be branch-local.

Let \(\Omega_+\) be the active plus interval in the local coordinate \(x=\log n/Q\). The needed statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\Omega_+,w)}
\left(
\frac{1}{m_{\can,+}},\mathbf C_R[x]
\right)
\ge
e^{-C R\log R}
\left\|
\frac{1}{m_{\can,+}}
\right\|_{L^2(\Omega_+,w)}.
}
\]

Since

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

the loss

\[
e^{-C R\log R}
\]

is polynomial in \(Q^{-1}\). This is the right scale.

A weaker absolute form is enough if the normalization of \(m_{\can,+}\) is already fixed:

\[
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x]
\right)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

---

## 2. Model case \(m_{\can,+}(x)=\kappa x^2\)

In the model,

\[
f(x)=\frac{1}{m_{\can,+}(x)}=\frac{1}{\kappa x^2}.
\]

Suppose \(\Omega_+\) is an interval whose distance to \(0\) is comparable to its length after branch rescaling. Then \(x^{-2}\) has a nearby pole, and polynomial approximation cannot be too good.

A direct divided-difference proof works.

For any polynomial \(P\in\mathbf C_R[x]\), choose \(R+2\) points

\[
x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}\in \Omega_+
\]

with pairwise spacing

\[
|x_i-x_j|\ge c|\Omega_+|/R.
\]

Since the \(R+1\)-st divided difference of \(P\) vanishes,

\[
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}](f-P)
=
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]f.
\]

For

\[
f(x)=x^{-2},
\]

Hermite–Genocchi gives

\[
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]f
=
\int_{\Delta_{R+1}}
f^{(R+1)}
\left(\sum t_ix_i\right)dt.
\]

And

\[
f^{(R+1)}(x)
=
(-1)^{R+1}(R+2)!\,x^{-R-3}.
\]

If all \(x_i\) lie on one side of the singularity and the interval does not cross \(0\), this derivative has fixed phase. Hence

\[
|[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]f|
\gtrsim
(R+2)!\,M^{-R-3},
\]

where

\[
M=\sup_{x\in\Omega_+}|x|.
\]

On the other hand,

\[
|[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}](f-P)|
\le
\frac{\max_i |f(x_i)-P(x_i)|}
{\prod_{i<j}|x_i-x_j|^{O(1)}},
\]

or more concretely the inverse Vandermonde bound gives loss at most

\[
\exp(O(R\log R))|\Omega_+|^{-R-1}.
\]

Therefore

\[
\max_i |f(x_i)-P(x_i)|
\ge
e^{-O(R\log R)}
\]

after branch normalization. Passing from pointwise samples to \(L^2\) on small neighborhoods around the \(x_i\) loses another \(e^{-O(R\log R)}\), still acceptable.

Thus

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(x^{-2},\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}.
}
\]

This gives the desired polynomial lower bound.

---

## 3. Perturbed model \(m_{\can,+}=\kappa x^2(1+\varepsilon)\)

Let

\[
m_{\can,+}(x)=\kappa x^2(1+\varepsilon(x)),
\]

and

\[
f(x)=\frac{1}{m_{\can,+}(x)}.
\]

Write

\[
f=f_0+\Delta f,
\qquad
f_0=\frac{1}{\kappa x^2}.
\]

The lower bound survives if

\[
\|\Delta f\|_{L^2(\Omega_+)}
\le
\frac12
\operatorname{dist}(f_0,\mathbf C_R[x]).
\]

A more robust high-derivative criterion is:

\[
\boxed{
\left|
\left(
f-f_0
\right)^{(R+1)}
\right|
\le
\eta
\left|
f_0^{(R+1)}
\right|
\quad
\text{on }\Omega_+,
\qquad \eta<1.
}
\]

Then the divided-difference argument still works because

\[
[f]_{R+1}
=
[f_0]_{R+1}+[f-f_0]_{R+1}
\]

and the perturbative divided difference cannot cancel the model one.

So the finite multiplier audit is:

\[
\boxed{
1/m_{\can,+}
=
(\kappa x^2)^{-1}
+
\text{perturbation whose }(R+1)\text{-st divided differences are smaller.}
}
\]

This is likely easier to prove than a direct global \(L^2\)-approximation estimate.

---

## 4. Active branch too far from the pole

There is a real failure mode.

If \(\Omega_+\) lies far from the zero of \(m_{\can,+}\), then \(1/m_{\can,+}\) may be analytic in a very large Bernstein ellipse around \(\Omega_+\). Degree-\(R\) polynomials could approximate it with error

\[
\rho^{-R},
\]

where \(\rho\) is large.

If

\[
\rho^R\gg Q^A,
\]

then the graph distance may be too small.

Therefore branch routing must ensure:

\[
\boxed{
\Omega_+
\text{ lies within polynomial conformal distance of a singularity/curvature zero of }m_{\can,+}.
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\log \rho \le O(\log R)
\]

is sufficient, because then

\[
\rho^{-R}\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}=Q^{-O(1)}.
\]

If this is not true, the pure-plus graph route cannot prove O2/O3 on that branch. That region must be routed differently or one must use another non-polynomial multiplier.

---

## 5. Central rescaling

Near a central or transition scale, write

\[
x=\delta y,
\]

or more generally around the relevant local singularity

\[
x=x_0+\delta y.
\]

If

\[
m_{\can,+}(x)\sim \kappa (x-x_0)^2,
\]

then

\[
\frac1{m_{\can,+}(x)}
\sim
\delta^{-2}\frac1{\kappa y^2}.
\]

The scalar factor \(\delta^{-2}\) is absorbed into normalization. The approximation problem is again

\[
\operatorname{dist}(y^{-2},\mathbf C_R[y]).
\]

If \(\delta\ge R^{-C}\), the change of variables introduces only polynomial losses:

\[
\delta^R\ge R^{-CR}=Q^{-O(1)}.
\]

Thus the graph theorem is stable under multiscale central routing at \(R\)-polynomial scales.

This reinforces the current conclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{central zones should be rescaled, not discarded.}
}
\]

---

## 6. What exactly must be proved about \(m_{\can,+}\)

The needed multiplier theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Canonical Quadratic Multiplier Theorem.}
}
\]

On every active plus O2/O3 branch, after branch normalization and possible rescaling, there exist \(\kappa\ne0\), a local coordinate \(z\), and a weight \(w\) such that

\[
m_{\can,+}(z)=\kappa z^2(1+\varepsilon(z)),
\]

with:

\[
|\kappa|\asymp Q^{O(1)},
\]

\[
1+\varepsilon(z)
\]

bounded above and below by powers of \(Q\), and

\[
\left|
\left(
\frac{1}{m_{\can,+}}-\frac{1}{\kappa z^2}
\right)^{(R+1)}
\right|
\le
\eta
\left|
\left(
\frac{1}{\kappa z^2}
\right)^{(R+1)}
\right|
\]

for some \(\eta<1\), or an equivalent divided-difference dominance condition.

Then

\[
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[z]
\right)
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}.
\]

This theorem is the finite analytic core of O2/O3.

---

## 7. Relationship to the near-pure ratio \(r(x)\)

The same quadratic model gives the branch substitution ratio:

\[
r(x)=\frac{m_{\can,-}(1-x)}{m_{\can,+}(x)}
\sim
\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}.
\]

So the graph non-polynomiality and the near-pure moment separation use the same multiplier structure.

This is an important connection:

\[
\boxed{
m_{\can,+}\sim x^2
\text{ gives both pure-plus determinant mass and fold-ratio non-lock.}
}
\]

Thus the O2/O3 proof can be organized around a single canonical multiplier theorem.

---

## 8. Current O2/O3 status

If the canonical quadratic multiplier theorem holds, then:

1. graph non-polynomiality gives pure-plus determinant mass;
2. \(R^C\)-normalization gives denominator control;
3. Christoffel spread gives substitution marginal spread;
4. \(r(x)=(1-x)^2/x^2\) gives fixed-degree near-pure separation;
5. branch-charge boundedness gives full determinant survival.

So the remaining O2/O3 problem is finite and analytic.

The major unresolved inputs are:

\[
\boxed{
m_{\can,+}\sim \kappa z^2(1+\varepsilon)
\text{ on active branches;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
R^C\text{-Riesz normalization;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
pure-plus exterior mass not lost to completion/tail sectors;}
}
\]

but the determinant-collision issue is no longer central.

---

## Final result of this attack

The pure-plus determinant mass follows from one finite analytic theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}.
}
\]

A sufficient and natural proof is:

\[
\boxed{
m_{\can,+}(x)=\kappa x^2(1+\varepsilon(x))
}
\]

with high divided-difference control of the perturbation.

This same quadratic multiplier structure also drives the near-pure substitution ratio

\[
r(x)=\frac{(1-x)^2}{x^2}.
\]

So O2/O3 now reduces to proving the canonical quadratic multiplier theorem on the active branch atlas.

## 1. The real invariant is a nearby double zero/pole

The graph obstruction is not specifically about the coordinate \(x\). It is about the reciprocal

\[
f=\frac1{m_{\can,+}}
\]

having a pole of order two at polynomial conformal distance from the active interval.

So the invariant condition should be:

\[
\boxed{
m_{\can,+}\text{ has an effective double zero }z_0
\text{ near the active plus interval.}
}
\]

More precisely, after local coordinate

\[
z=x-z_0,
\]

we need

\[
m_{\can,+}(x)
=
\kappa z^2 u(z),
\qquad
u(0)=1,
\]

where \(u\) is nonvanishing and slowly varying on the active branch window.

Then

\[
\frac1{m_{\can,+}}
=
\kappa^{-1}z^{-2}u(z)^{-1}.
\]

The pole \(z^{-2}\) is the source of non-polynomiality. The unit \(u^{-1}\) is harmless if it does not cancel the high divided differences.

---

## 2. Checkable conditions

A practical finite theorem can use these hypotheses on a normalized active interval \(\Omega\):

### H1. Effective double zero

There is a point \(z_0\) at distance

\[
\operatorname{dist}(z_0,\Omega)\le R^C|\Omega|
\]

such that

\[
m_{\can,+}(x)=\kappa (x-z_0)^2u(x),
\]

with

\[
Q^{-C}\le |\kappa|\le Q^C.
\]

### H2. Unit control

\[
Q^{-C}\le |u(x)|\le Q^C
\]

on a complex neighborhood of \(\Omega\) of polynomial relative size.

### H3. High divided-difference control

For every \(R+2\)-tuple

\[
x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}\in\Omega
\]

with polynomial separation,

\[
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]
\left(
z^{-2}u^{-1}-z^{-2}
\right)
\]

is at most a fixed fraction of

\[
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]z^{-2}.
\]

A sufficient derivative form is

\[
\left|
\left(z^{-2}(u^{-1}-1)\right)^{(R+1)}
\right|
\le
\eta
\left|
(z^{-2})^{(R+1)}
\right|,
\qquad
\eta<1.
\]

This is stronger than necessary, but easy to audit.

Under H1–H3,

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}\|1/m_{\can,+}\|_{\mathrm{branch}}.
}
\]

---

## 3. Proof skeleton

Let

\[
f(x)=1/m_{\can,+}(x).
\]

For any \(P\in \mathbf C_R[x]\), choose \(R+2\) polynomially separated points

\[
x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}\in\Omega.
\]

Since

\[
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]P=0,
\]

we have

\[
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}](f-P)
=
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]f.
\]

By H3,

\[
|[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]f|
\ge
c|\kappa|^{-1}
|[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]z^{-2}|.
\]

For

\[
z^{-2},
\]

Hermite–Genocchi gives

\[
[x_0,\ldots,x_{R+1}]z^{-2}
=
\int_{\Delta_{R+1}}
(-1)^{R+1}(R+2)!
\left(\sum t_i(x_i-z_0)\right)^{-R-3}dt.
\]

If the active branch stays on one side of \(z_0\), there is no phase cancellation, and the magnitude is bounded below by

\[
e^{-O(R\log R)}
\]

after normalization.

The inverse Vandermonde bound converts the divided-difference lower bound into a pointwise lower bound for \(f-P\) at one of the \(x_i\), with loss

\[
e^{O(R\log R)}.
\]

Thickening \(x_i\) into small intervals and using Markov/Bernstein control converts the pointwise lower bound into an \(L^2\) lower bound, losing another

\[
e^{O(R\log R)}.
\]

Thus

\[
\operatorname{dist}(f,\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}.
\]

Since

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

this is polynomial in \(Q^{-1}\).

---

## 4. Failure modes

There are exactly four serious ways this theorem can fail.

### F1. No nearby double zero

If \(m_{\can,+}\) is nonzero and analytic far from the active branch, then \(1/m_{\can,+}\) can be exponentially well approximated by degree-\(R\) polynomials.

Then pure-plus determinant mass may be too small.

### F2. Wrong zero order

If

\[
m_{\can,+}(z)\sim \kappa z
\]

instead of \(\kappa z^2\), the reciprocal is \(z^{-1}\). That is still non-polynomial, but the divided-difference and determinant coefficient structure changes.

If

\[
m_{\can,+}(z)\sim \kappa z^k,
\]

then the graph theorem should use \(z^{-k}\). Any fixed \(k\ge1\) is probably workable, but the O2/O3 determinant coefficient formula must match the actual order.

So the finite audit must confirm the order.

### F3. Unit \(u\) cancels the pole signature

If \(u^{-1}\) has high derivatives arranged to cancel the \(z^{-2}\) divided differences, the model proof fails.

This is unlikely if \(u\) is slowly varying, but it must be bounded by H3.

### F4. Active branch crosses the pole

If \(\Omega\) crosses \(z_0\), \(1/m_{\can,+}\) is not in ordinary \(L^2\) without a principal-value or weighted interpretation.

That region should be routed to the transition/singular chart, not ordinary plus O2/O3.

---

## 5. Interaction with multiscale fold routing

If the active region is central, use

\[
x=z_0+\delta y.
\]

Then

\[
m_{\can,+}(x)=\kappa\delta^2 y^2u(z_0+\delta y),
\]

and

\[
1/m_{\can,+}
=
\kappa^{-1}\delta^{-2}y^{-2}u^{-1}.
\]

The scalar factor \(\delta^{-2}\) cancels in normalized graph distance.

The only loss is the polynomial rescaling of divided differences. If

\[
\delta\ge R^{-C},
\]

then

\[
\delta^R\ge R^{-CR}=Q^{-O(1)}.
\]

Thus central routing is compatible with graph non-polynomiality.

This confirms the current branch philosophy:

\[
\boxed{
\text{ordinary/transition/singular are scale choices for the same pole model.}
}
\]

---

## 6. The finite audit to request from the multiplier thread

The multiplier thread should now answer:

1. What is \(m_{\can,+}\) exactly in the plus graph model?

2. Does it have a zero \(z_0\) of order exactly two in the branch coordinate?

3. Is the active plus branch within polynomial conformal distance of \(z_0\)?

4. Is the unit

\[
u=m_{\can,+}/(\kappa z^2)
\]

nonvanishing with polynomial derivative/divided-difference control?

5. Does the singular/transition routing remove intervals crossing \(z_0\)?

If yes, O2/O3 graph non-polynomiality is essentially Green.

---

## 7. Consequence for the O2/O3 chain

With the canonical quadratic multiplier theorem in place:

\[
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])\ge Q^{-A},
\]

we get:

\[
\int |D_{-n}|^2W_{\mathrm{bd}}
\ge Q^{-A}\int W_{\mathrm{bd}}.
\]

Then the previously established chain applies:

\[
\text{pure-plus mass}
\Rightarrow
\text{substitution marginal support}
\Rightarrow
\text{finite moment separation for }r(x)
\Rightarrow
\text{near-pure Schur complement}
\Rightarrow
\text{full determinant survival}
\Rightarrow
\mathrm{AggFrame}_{23}.
\]

Thus the determinant-collision problem has been replaced by a finite analytic multiplier audit.

---

## Final result of this attack

The exact O2/O3 finite-symbol theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
m_{\can,+}(x)=\kappa (x-z_0)^2u(x)
}
\]

with \(z_0\) polynomially close to the active branch, \(u\) nonvanishing and slowly varying, and branch routing excluding intervals that cross \(z_0\).

Under those conditions,

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])
\ge e^{-O(R\log R)}.
}
\]

This is the finite analytic core needed for the fixed-\(\alpha\) O2/O3 route.

for the lower \(Y12\)-fiber graph slope

# Agent 5 — moving-wall finite type modulo coherent amplitude spaces

We assume \(Y12\) survives the refined quotient and that D4 gives the effective rank-one wall

\[
\ell_{\mathrm{raw}}(\Gamma)
=
(\beta_9-\rho_Yc_7)\Gamma_G+\alpha_9\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\ell_{\mathrm{raw}}(\Gamma)
=
\alpha_9
\left(
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G
\right).
\]

The wall theorem is not just a sublevel theorem for a scalar function. If finite-dimensional coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitude functions are present, the correct object is the distance of this wall amplitude from that coherent amplitude space.

---

## 1. Wall theorem without coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitudes

If there is no coherent \(Y12\)-amplitude space, the required theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\int
\min\left(
Q^A|H|^2|\alpha_9|^2
|\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G|^2,
1
\right)
\,d\nu
\ge
Q^{-B}\nu(\mathcal C).
}
\]

After dyadically freezing

\[
|H|^2|\alpha_9|^2,
\]

this reduces to

\[
\boxed{
\nu\left(
|\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G|<\varepsilon
\right)
\le
Q^C\varepsilon^\theta\nu(\mathcal C).
}
\]

This is the clean moving-wall anti-concentration theorem.

---

## 2. Wall theorem with coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitudes

Let

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

be the finite-dimensional coherent \(Y12\)-amplitude space coming from fixed/coherent \(E_2^Y\), parameter graph terms, or other legitimate actual-source amplitude effects.

Then the correct theorem is

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\nu)}
\left(
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G),
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\right)^2
\ge
Q^{-A}
\left\|
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G)
\right\|_{L^2(\nu)}^2.
}
\]

Equivalently, for every

\[
\psi\in\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}},
\]

one needs

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G)-\psi
\right\|_{L^2(\nu)}^2
\ge
Q^{-A}
\left\|
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G)
\right\|_{L^2(\nu)}^2.
}
\]

This is the amplitude-level analogue of the \(Y12\)-fiber separation. It is the correct replacement for a naive scalar wall sublevel estimate when coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitudes are finite-dimensional but nonzero.

If \(\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}\) is locally free at extraction scale, this theorem is false: the coherent amplitude can absorb the D4 wall. That is the fatal \(E_2^Y\) case.

---

## 3. Reduction on \(|\Gamma_G|\) large

Set

\[
A=\Gamma_G,\qquad B=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

On the region

\[
|A|\ge Q^{-C},
\]

define

\[
R_\Gamma=\frac{B}{A}.
\]

Then

\[
B+\rho_\Gamma A
=
A(R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma).
\]

The wall sublevel

\[
|B+\rho_\Gamma A|<\varepsilon
\]

becomes

\[
|R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma|
<
\varepsilon |A|^{-1}.
\]

So anti-concentration follows from finite type of

\[
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma.
\]

A first derivative witness is

\[
\boxed{
W_{\ell}
=
A B'-A'B+\rho_\Gamma' A^2.
}
\]

Indeed,

\[
A^2(R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma)'
=
A^2\left(\frac{B}{A}\right)'
+
A^2\rho_\Gamma'
=
A B'-A'B+\rho_\Gamma'A^2.
\]

Therefore, if on most of the smooth post-Gate/O2/O3 measure one has

\[
|A|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

and

\[
|W_\ell|\ge Q^{-C}|A|^2,
\]

then

\[
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\]

has polynomial finite type, and standard one-dimensional sublevel estimates give

\[
\nu\left(
|B+\rho_\Gamma A|<\varepsilon
\right)
\le
Q^C\varepsilon^\theta\nu(\mathcal C).
\]

More generally, if the first \(k\le k_0=O(1)\) derivatives of \(R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma\) are not all small, the same conclusion holds with a different exponent \(\theta\).

---

## 4. The \(|\Gamma_G|\) small region

The reduction by \(A=\Gamma_G\) fails where

\[
|\Gamma_G|
\]

is small. There we need a joint sublevel estimate:

\[
\boxed{
\nu\left(
|\Gamma_G|<\varepsilon,\ 
|\Gamma_{G,2}|<\varepsilon
\right)
\le
Q^C\varepsilon^\theta\nu(\mathcal C).
}
\]

This is a genuine two-coordinate wall-escape input.

If

\[
|\Gamma_G|
\]

is small but

\[
|\Gamma_{G,2}|\ge Q^{-C},
\]

then the wall is automatically non-small because

\[
B+\rho_\Gamma A
=
\Gamma_{G,2}+O(Q^C\varepsilon).
\]

So the only problematic small-\(A\) region is where both \(\Gamma_G\) and \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) are small.

Thus the wall theorem splits cleanly:

\[
\boxed{
|\Gamma_G|\text{ large}
\Rightarrow
\text{finite type of }R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma;
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\Gamma_G|\text{ small}
\Rightarrow
\text{joint sublevel for }(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}).
}
\]

---

## 5. Finite-dimensional coherent amplitude space

If

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\neq0
\]

but is finite-dimensional, choose a basis

\[
\psi_1,\ldots,\psi_M,
\qquad M\le Q^C.
\]

Then the wall theorem is a Schur-distance estimate:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
F_{\mathrm{wall}},
\operatorname{span}\{\psi_1,\ldots,\psi_M\}
\right)^2
\ge
Q^{-A}
\|F_{\mathrm{wall}}\|^2,
}
\]

where

\[
F_{\mathrm{wall}}
=
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G).
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\delta_{\mathrm{wall}}^2
=
\|F_{\mathrm{wall}}\|^2
-
b^*G_\psi^\dagger b
\ge
Q^{-A}\|F_{\mathrm{wall}}\|^2,
\]

with

\[
(G_\psi)_{ij}=\langle\psi_i,\psi_j\rangle_{L^2(\nu)},
\]

\[
b_i=\langle F_{\mathrm{wall}},\psi_i\rangle_{L^2(\nu)}.
\]

If this finite-dimensional Schur residual is polynomially bounded below, the coherent \(E_2^Y\) amplitudes do not absorb the D4 wall.

This is the precise amplitude-level analogue of the \(Y12\)-fiber Schur test.

---

## 6. Wall ODE if finite type fails

If

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G\equiv0
\]

on a large set, then the wall is trapped by a fourth-order differential relation.

Using the coordinator’s convention, wall vanishing gives

\[
\boxed{
q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
-
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
+
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3.
}
\]

Here the sign convention depends on the definitions of

\[
\Gamma_G,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

The important structural point is that wall failure imposes a fourth-order ODE with coefficient

\[
\rho_\Gamma.
\]

If

\[
\rho_\Gamma
\]

is low-order—meaning it belongs to the low Gram/whitening algebra and does not depend on \(q^{(4)}\), \(\Gamma_{G,2}\), or order-nine source data—then this ODE is a genuine finite-type/trapping condition, not a tautology.

Thus the wall theorem depends crucially on Agent 4’s low-order audit:

\[
\boxed{
\rho_\Gamma\in\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}},
\qquad
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0.
}
\]

If \(\rho_\Gamma\) depends on \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) or \(q^{(4)}\), the wall equation may become tautological, and the rank-one channel loses force.

---

## 7. The theorem needed

The clean theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{MovingWallFiniteTypeModuloCoherence.}
}
\]

Assume:

1. \(Y12\) survives and produces

\[
F_{\mathrm{wall}}
=
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G).
\]

2. \(\rho_\Gamma\) is low-order:

\[
\rho_\Gamma\in\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}},
\qquad
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0.
\]

3. Any coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitude space

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

is finite-dimensional with polynomial conditioning.

4. On the post-Gate/O2/O3 smooth measure, either:

\[
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\]

has polynomial finite type on \(|\Gamma_G|\ge Q^{-C}\), and the joint sublevel set

\[
|\Gamma_G|,\ |\Gamma_{G,2}|<\varepsilon
\]

has polynomially small measure; or equivalently the Schur distance of \(F_{\mathrm{wall}}\) to \(\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}\) is polynomially nonzero.

Then

\[
\boxed{
\int
\min\left(
Q^A
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G),
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\right)^2,
1
\right)d\nu
\ge Q^{-B}\nu(\mathcal C).
}
\]

This supplies the rank-one wall energy for downstream extraction.

---

## 8. What can fail

There are three distinct failure modes.

### Failure 1: low-order audit fails

If

\[
\rho_\Gamma
\]

depends on \(\Gamma_{G,2}\), \(q^{(4)}\), or actual order-nine data, the wall equation can become tautological.

Then D4 has no usable finite-type obstruction.

### Failure 2: coherent amplitude absorption

If

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

contains arbitrary local multiples of

\[
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G),
\]

then the wall amplitude is absorbed. This is the locally free \(E_2^Y\) failure.

### Failure 3: genuine wall ODE trapping

If the canonical source actually satisfies the fourth-order wall ODE on a large set with low-order \(\rho_\Gamma\), then the rank-one wall has no mass. That would be a new structural obstruction and must be routed separately or used as an alternative contradiction.

---

## Final result of this attack

The wall theorem is now a finite-type statement modulo coherent amplitude spaces:

\[
\boxed{
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G)
\notin
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
}
\]

with polynomial quantitative distance.

On \(|\Gamma_G|\) large, this reduces to finite type of

\[
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma,
\qquad
R_\Gamma=\Gamma_{G,2}/\Gamma_G.
\]

On \(|\Gamma_G|\) small, it requires joint anti-concentration of

\[
(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}).
\]

If it fails identically, the source is trapped by the fourth-order wall ODE

\[
q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
-
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
+
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3,
\]

which is only a meaningful obstruction if \(\rho_\Gamma\) is genuinely low-order.

## 1. Reduce the wall theorem to a finite-type sublevel theorem

Set

\[
A=\Gamma_G,\qquad B=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Then

\[
\ell_{\mathrm{eff}}(\Gamma)
=
B+\rho_\Gamma A.
\]

On the region

\[
|A|\ge Q^{-C},
\]

define

\[
R_\Gamma=\frac BA.
\]

Then

\[
\ell_{\mathrm{eff}}(\Gamma)
=
A(R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma).
\]

So the wall sublevel problem reduces to

\[
|R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma|<\varepsilon |A|^{-1}.
\]

Since \(|A|\ge Q^{-C}\), it is enough to prove a sublevel estimate for the scalar function

\[
\Phi:=R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma.
\]

The first derivative witness is

\[
\boxed{
W_\ell
=
AB'-A'B+\rho_\Gamma' A^2.
}
\]

Indeed,

\[
A^2\Phi'
=
A^2\left(\frac BA+\rho_\Gamma\right)'
=
AB'-A'B+\rho_\Gamma'A^2.
\]

Thus if

\[
|W_\ell|\ge Q^{-C}|A|^2
\]

on most of the relevant measure, then

\[
|\Phi'|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

A standard one-dimensional sublevel bound gives

\[
\nu\{|\Phi|<\varepsilon\}
\le Q^C\varepsilon\,\nu(\mathcal C)
\]

on each monotonicity interval, up to polynomially many intervals.

Therefore

\[
\nu\{|\ell_{\mathrm{eff}}(\Gamma)|<\varepsilon\}
\le Q^C\varepsilon^\theta\nu(\mathcal C)
\]

for some fixed \(\theta>0\).

---

## 2. Higher finite-type version

The first derivative may vanish at isolated or low-dimensional loci. The robust condition is finite type.

Assume there exists \(k\le k_0=O(1)\) such that

\[
\left|
\partial_m^k
\left(
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\right)
\right|
\ge Q^{-C}
\]

on each atom of a polynomial partition of the post-Gate/O2O3 measure.

Then the usual finite-type sublevel estimate gives

\[
\nu\{|R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma|<\varepsilon\}
\le
Q^C\varepsilon^{1/k_0}\nu(\mathcal C).
\]

Hence

\[
\boxed{
\int
\min\left(
Q^A|\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G|^2,
1
\right)
d\nu
\ge Q^{-B}\nu(\mathcal C).
}
\]

So the wall theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\text{ is not polynomially flat on the Stack C2 mass.}
}
\]

This is the precise finite-type wall input.

---

## 3. The small-\(\Gamma_G\) branch

The ratio

\[
R_\Gamma=\Gamma_{G,2}/\Gamma_G
\]

is only useful where

\[
|\Gamma_G|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

On the complementary region

\[
|\Gamma_G|<Q^{-C},
\]

the wall is

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G
=
\Gamma_{G,2}+O(Q^C|\Gamma_G|).
\]

So if

\[
|\Gamma_{G,2}|\ge Q^{-C'}
\]

then the wall is automatically non-small.

The only dangerous set is

\[
|\Gamma_G|<Q^{-C},
\qquad
|\Gamma_{G,2}|<Q^{-C'}.
\]

Thus the second wall input is a joint sublevel theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\nu\{
|\Gamma_G|<\varepsilon,\,
|\Gamma_{G,2}|<\varepsilon
\}
\le Q^C\varepsilon^\theta\nu(\mathcal C).
}
\]

This is weaker than arbitrary two-dimensional anisotropy. It is only needed on the small-\(\Gamma_G\) branch.

---

## 4. Coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitude spaces

If there is a finite-dimensional coherent \(Y12\)-amplitude space

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}},
\]

then the wall theorem is not a scalar sublevel statement. It is a distance statement:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\nu)}
\left(
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G),
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\right)^2
\ge
Q^{-A}
\left\|
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G)
\right\|_{L^2(\nu)}^2.
}
\]

If \(\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}\) has dimension \(M\le Q^C\), choose a basis

\[
\psi_1,\ldots,\psi_M.
\]

Then the required condition is the Schur residual

\[
\delta_{\mathrm{wall}}^2
=
\|F_{\mathrm{wall}}\|^2
-
b^*G^\dagger b
\ge
Q^{-A}\|F_{\mathrm{wall}}\|^2,
\]

where

\[
F_{\mathrm{wall}}
=
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G),
\]

\[
G_{ij}=\langle\psi_i,\psi_j\rangle_{L^2(\nu)},
\]

\[
b_i=\langle F_{\mathrm{wall}},\psi_i\rangle_{L^2(\nu)}.
\]

So coherent \(E_2^Y\) does not automatically kill the wall. It only kills it if the wall amplitude lies too close to that finite-dimensional coherent amplitude space.

---

## 5. Low-order nature of \(\rho_\Gamma\)

The finite-type wall theorem is meaningful only if

\[
\rho_\Gamma
=
\frac{\beta_9-\rho_Yc_7}{\alpha_9}
\]

is genuinely low-order.

The required condition is

\[
\boxed{
\rho_\Gamma\in\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}},
\qquad
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0.
}
\]

Here \(\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}}\) is generated by lower Gram/whitening variables, fixed carrier constants, and low-order scalar invariants such as

\[
g=\frac{q_0}{\pi},
\qquad
h=\frac1\pi\left(\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''_{\mathrm{can}}\right),
\]

but **not** by

\[
q^{(4)},\quad
\Gamma_{G,2},\quad
s_9^Y,\quad
\text{or actual order-nine source data}.
\]

If \(\rho_\Gamma\) depends on \(\Gamma_{G,2}\), the wall equation may become tautological.

Thus the next finite audit is:

\[
\boxed{
\rho_Y,\beta_9,\alpha_9,c_7
\text{ are low-order, and }
\alpha_9\text{ is polynomially nonzero.}
}
\]

---

## 6. Wall failure gives an ODE trap

If the wall vanishes,

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G=0,
\]

then, using the coordinator’s current normalization, the scalar source satisfies a fourth-order relation of the form

\[
\boxed{
q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
-
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
+
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3.
}
\]

This is not automatically a contradiction. It becomes useful only if

\[
\rho_\Gamma
\]

is low-order and independent of \(q^{(4)}\). Then this is a genuine finite-type ODE constraint.

So persistent wall failure routes to a new model-space statement:

\[
\boxed{
q\text{ is trapped near a low-order fourth-order ODE model.}
}
\]

That can be attacked by the same model-space transversality philosophy used in Gate–1 and O2/O3.

---

## 7. Final wall theorem

The clean theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{MovingWallFiniteTypeModuloCoherence.}
}
\]

Assume:

1. \(Y12\) survives the quotient, giving the wall

\[
F_{\mathrm{wall}}
=
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G).
\]

2. \(\rho_\Gamma\in\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}}\) and

\[
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0.
\]

3. Any coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitude space

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

has polynomial dimension and conditioning.

4. On \(|\Gamma_G|\ge Q^{-C}\), the scalar function

\[
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\]

has polynomial finite type modulo \(\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}\).

5. On \(|\Gamma_G|<Q^{-C}\), the joint sublevel of

\[
(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2})
\]

is polynomially small.

Then

\[
\boxed{
\int
\min\left(
Q^A
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G),
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\right)^2,
1
\right)
d\nu
\ge Q^{-B}\nu(\mathcal C).
}
\]

This supplies the wall mass needed for the rank-one \(Y12\) D4 channel.

---

## 8. Current conclusion

The moving-wall problem is no longer vague. It has become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite type of }
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\text{ plus joint sublevel of }(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}),
}
\]

possibly modulo a finite-dimensional coherent amplitude space.

The two highest-priority finite checks are now:

\[
\boxed{
\rho_\Gamma
=
(\beta_9-\rho_Yc_7)/\alpha_9
\text{ is low-order;}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\text{ is not trapped near zero on the post-Gate/O2O3 mass.}
}
\]

If both hold, the \(Y12\) rank-one wall is viable.

## 1. Define the wall residual

Let

\[
A=\Gamma_G,\qquad B=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Define

\[
W_{\mathrm{wall}}
=
B+\rho_\Gamma A.
\]

The wall factor is

\[
|H|^2|\alpha_9|^2|W_{\mathrm{wall}}|^2.
\]

The desired anti-concentration is

\[
\nu\{|W_{\mathrm{wall}}|<\varepsilon\}
\le Q^C\varepsilon^\theta\nu(\mathcal C)
\]

after dyadic normalization.

The finite-type witness is

\[
W_\ell
=
AB'-A'B+\rho_\Gamma' A^2.
\]

Since

\[
A^2\left(\frac BA+\rho_\Gamma\right)'
=
AB'-A'B+\rho_\Gamma'A^2,
\]

we have

\[
W_\ell=A^2(R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma)'.
\]

So on \(|A|\ge Q^{-C}\), a lower bound on \(W_\ell\) gives finite type of the wall residual.

---

## 2. Finite-type-or-trap dichotomy

On a local atom \(I\), one of the following must hold.

### Regular finite-type branch

There exists \(1\le k\le k_0=O(1)\) such that

\[
\left|
\partial_m^k
(R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma)
\right|
\ge Q^{-C}
\]

on a polynomial fraction of \(I\).

Then standard finite-type sublevel estimates give

\[
\nu_I\{|W_{\mathrm{wall}}|<\varepsilon\}
\le Q^C\varepsilon^{1/k_0}\nu_I(I).
\]

This gives the required wall mass.

### ODE-trap branch

For every \(1\le k\le k_0\),

\[
\left|
\partial_m^k
(R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma)
\right|
\le Q^{-C}
\]

on most of \(I\).

Then

\[
R_\Gamma+\rho_\Gamma
\]

is nearly flat. Equivalently,

\[
W_{\mathrm{wall}}
=
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G
\]

is trapped near zero to high order. This means the local scalar source \(q\) is close to a finite-dimensional ODE model.

Thus the wall theorem reduces to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite type}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\text{ODE trap.}
}
\]

The finite-type branch is good. The ODE-trap branch must either be excluded or routed into a separate contradiction.

---

## 3. The ODE trap

Wall vanishing gives, under the current normalization,

\[
\boxed{
q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
-
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
+
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3.
}
\]

Write this as

\[
\mathcal W(q)
=
q^{(4)}
-
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
+
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
-
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3.
\]

Then

\[
W_{\mathrm{wall}}=0
\]

is equivalent to

\[
\mathcal W(q)=0
\]

up to harmless normalization constants.

If finite type fails, then not only

\[
\mathcal W(q)\approx0,
\]

but also

\[
\partial_m^j\mathcal W(q)\approx0,
\qquad
0\le j\le k_0.
\]

This constrains the higher jet

\[
q^{(4)},q^{(5)},\ldots,q^{(4+k_0)}
\]

as functions of the lower jet and of \(\rho_\Gamma\) and its derivatives.

If

\[
\rho_\Gamma\in\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}}
\]

and

\[
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0,
\]

then this is a genuine low-order ODE model. It is not tautological.

So the wall failure branch is a new model-space condition:

\[
\boxed{
q\text{ lies near the solution manifold of a low-order fourth-order ODE.}
}
\]

---

## 4. Why low-order \(\rho_\Gamma\) is essential

If

\[
\rho_\Gamma
\]

depends on

\[
q^{(4)},\quad \Gamma_{G,2},\quad s_9^Y,
\]

then the equation

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G=0
\]

may be an identity disguised as a wall equation.

In that case the ODE trap is meaningless.

So the wall theorem requires:

\[
\boxed{
\rho_\Gamma
\in
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}},
\qquad
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0.
}
\]

Here

\[
\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}}
\]

may depend on lower Gram/whitening variables such as

\[
g=\frac{q_0}{\pi},
\qquad
h=\frac1\pi\left(\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''_{\mathrm{can}}\right),
\]

and fixed carrier constants, but not order-nine or fourth-derivative data.

This is the guardrail that makes the ODE trap a genuine obstruction rather than a tautology.

---

## 5. How to use the ODE trap

There are three possible outcomes.

### A. ODE trap excluded by source arithmetic

Prove that the canonical source cannot satisfy

\[
\mathcal W(q)\approx0
\]

and its first \(k_0\) derivatives on a polynomial-weight set.

Then wall anti-concentration follows.

This is the strongest result.

### B. ODE trap implies O2/O3 or Gate–1 directly

The ODE trap constrains

\[
q^{(4)}
\]

in terms of lower derivatives. This may force a nontrivial finite jet relation that feeds O2/O3 directly, or it may force local \(L^2\) energy of \(q''_{\mathrm{can}}\).

Then wall failure is not fatal; it routes to an alternate branch.

### C. ODE trap is a real escape

If the canonical source can genuinely lie near this ODE model on large mass and the rest of the stack cannot contradict it, the rank-one \(Y12\) wall is insufficient.

Then one needs either:

\[
\text{rank two D4},
\]

or a higher-order residual beyond \(Y12\).

---

## 6. Interaction with coherent \(E_2^Y\)-amplitude space

If a finite-dimensional coherent amplitude space

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

is present, then wall failure means

\[
H\alpha_9 W_{\mathrm{wall}}
\]

lies near

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}.
\]

The ODE trap becomes inhomogeneous:

\[
\mathcal W(q)
=
\psi(m)
\]

for some

\[
\psi\in \mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

after normalization.

Thus the correct trap model is

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal W(q)\in \mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}.
}
\]

If

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

is finite-dimensional and low complexity, this is still a finite-dimensional model-space condition.

The wall anti-concentration theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}_{L^2(\nu)}
\left(
\mathcal W(q),
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\right)
\ge Q^{-A}
\|\mathcal W(q)\|_{L^2(\nu)}.
}
\]

If \(\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}\) is locally free, the model becomes too large and the wall dies.

---

## 7. Finite-type theorem statement

A clean theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{WallFiniteTypeOrLowOrderODETrap.}
}
\]

Assume:

1. \(Y12\) survives and gives the effective wall

\[
W_{\mathrm{wall}}
=
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G.
\]

2. \(\rho_\Gamma\in\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}}\) and

\[
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0.
\]

3. Any coherent \(Y12\)-amplitude space

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

is finite-dimensional and polynomially conditioned.

Then either:

### Regular wall branch

\[
W_{\mathrm{wall}}
\]

has polynomial finite type modulo

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}},
\]

and the wall anti-concentration theorem holds;

or

### Trap branch

\[
q
\]

is trapped near the low-order inhomogeneous fourth-order ODE model

\[
q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
-
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
+
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3
+
\psi,
\qquad
\psi\in\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}.
\]

This trap branch must then be excluded or routed.

---

## 8. What is now missing

The next mathematical audit is not generic wall anti-concentration. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the low-order ODE trap occur on a Stack C2-good set?}
}
\]

More explicitly:

\[
\boxed{
q^{(4)}
-
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
+
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
-
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3
\]

cannot lie in

\[
\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

to high order on polynomial mass.

This is a model-space distance theorem.

---

## Final result of this attack

The moving wall has a clean dichotomy:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite type}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\text{low-order ODE trap.}
}
\]

The rank-one \(Y12\) D4 route succeeds if the ODE trap is impossible or routes to a separate contradiction.

The key finite prerequisite remains:

\[
\boxed{
\rho_\Gamma
=
\frac{\beta_9-\rho_Yc_7}{\alpha_9}
\text{ is genuinely low-order.}

Once this is secured, wall failure is no longer vague; it is a precise fourth-order ODE model that can be attacked directly.

Continuing Agent 5 — analyze the low-order ODE trap left by moving-wall failure.

I’ll keep the current variable names:

\[
\rho_\Gamma=\frac{\beta_9-\rho_Yc_7}{\alpha_9},

\[

\[
\ell_{\mathrm{eff}}(\Gamma)
=

\]

The previous result was the dichotomy:

\[
\boxed{
\text{wall finite type}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\text{low-order fourth-order ODE trap.}
}
\]

Now the task is to determine whether the ODE-trap branch is itself contradictory or whether it becomes a genuine escape.

---

## 1. The trap equation

Wall failure means

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G\approx 0.
\]

Using the current normalization, this gives the fourth-order model

\[
\boxed{
q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
-
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
+
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3
}

or, if coherent \(Y12\)-amplitude terms are present,

q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
-
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
+
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3
+
\psi,
\qquad
\psi\in\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}.
}

Define the residual

\[
\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)
=
q^{(4)}
-
6(1-q_0^2+\rho_\Gamma)q''
+
36\rho_\Gamma q_0
-
12\rho_\Gamma q_0^3.
\]

Then the trap is

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)\in \mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
}
\]

to polynomial precision on a polynomial-weight Stack C2-good set.

---

## 2. This trap is not automatically contradictory

A fourth-order ODE can have many nonzero local solutions. Gate–1 only gives

\[
\int_I|q''_{\can}|^2\ge Q^{-A},
\]

and that is compatible with a fourth-order ODE. O2/O3 gives finite-frame nondegeneracy of the local source, but that also need not contradict an ODE relation.

So wall failure is not automatically impossible.

The ODE trap becomes useful only if one can prove one of:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Trap solutions are incompatible with actual canonical EF source structure;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Trap solutions force another D4-normal residual at higher order;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Trap solutions form a low-dimensional coherent model, and actual source cannot concentrate there;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Trap branch can be routed to a separate finite-type/nonflatness theorem.}
}
\]

Thus persistent wall failure creates a new branch. It does not close the proof by itself.

---

## 3. The trap is a low-dimensional jet model if \(\rho_\Gamma\) is low-order

Assume

\[
\rho_\Gamma\in\mathcal A_{\mathrm{low}},
\qquad
\partial_{\Gamma_{G,2}}\rho_\Gamma=0.
\]

If \(\rho_\Gamma\) depends only on lower variables such as

\[
q_0,\quad q'',\quad g,\quad h,
\]

and fixed carrier constants, then the trap equation expresses

\[
q^{(4)}
\]

as a function of lower data:

\[
q^{(4)}=F(q_0,q'',\rho_\Gamma)+\psi.
\]

Differentiating gives recurrences for higher derivatives:

\[
q^{(5)}
=
F_{q_0}q_0'
+
F_{q''}q'''
+
F_{\rho}\rho_\Gamma'
+
\psi',
\]

and similarly for \(q^{(6)},q^{(7)},\ldots\).

Thus, modulo the finite-dimensional coherent forcing space \(\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}\), the higher jet is determined by a small set of lower variables:

\[
q_0,\quad q_0',\quad q'',\quad q'''.
\]

This is a finite-dimensional ODE model.

So the trap branch is a **model-space concentration** statement:

\[
\boxed{
J_{\le K}(q)
\text{ lies near the finite ODE jet manifold }
\mathcal M_{\mathrm{ODE}}(\rho_\Gamma,\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}).
}
\]

This is the same structural pattern as the rest of the proof: distance from a model space.

---

## 4. Trap branch gives a new possible separating residual

Because the trap determines \(q^{(4)}\), the next natural residual is order five or higher:

\[
\mathcal W_\Gamma^{(1)}(q)
=
\partial_m\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)
-
\Pi_{\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}}\partial_m\mathcal W_\Gamma(q).
\]

If

\[
\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)\in\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

but

\[
\partial_m\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)
\notin
\partial_m\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
+
T\mathcal M_{\mathrm{ODE}},
\]

then the wall failure is not stable. It creates a higher-order normal direction.

This suggests a hierarchy:

\[
\boxed{
Y12\text{ rank-one wall}
\rightarrow
\text{if trapped, differentiate}
\rightarrow
\text{higher-order residual}
}
\]

So the ODE branch may not be fatal. It may simply move the forbidden channel from order nine to a higher residual.

This is analogous to increasing D4 order, but now in a targeted way: only on the wall-trapped branch.

---

## 5. Interaction with O2/O3

O2/O3 already says the local augmented matrix is nondegenerate. If the ODE trap imposes

\[
q^{(4)}=F(q_0,q''),
\]

then some columns/rows of the augmented matrix may become dependent. But O2/O3 is built from

\[
m_{\can}
\]

and the graph non-polynomiality of

\[
1/m_{\can}.
\]

These are not obviously incompatible.

However, if

\[
\rho_\Gamma
\]

is a function of the same low Gram variables defining \(m_{\can}\), the ODE trap may force \(1/m_{\can}\) into a lower-degree rational model. That would interact with the O2/O3 graph theorem.

A possible theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)\approx0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
1/m_{\can,+}
\text{ has improved polynomial approximability}
}
\]

or the opposite:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)\approx0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\operatorname{dist}(1/m_{\can,+},\mathbf C_R[x])
\text{ remains nonzero and yields a contradiction with the wall model.}
}
\]

At present, this relation is not proved. So ODE-trap handling remains a new task.

---

## 6. Interaction with Gate–1

Gate–1 is local \(L^2\) source energy:

\[
\int_I|q''_{\can}|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The ODE trap allows nonzero \(q''\). Therefore Gate–1 does not kill it.

But Gate–1 plus the ODE trap may imply a **controlled solution space**. If the ODE model has low dimension and the observation windows are thick, one can attempt an observability theorem for the ODE solutions:

\[
\|q''\|_{L^2(W_{\obs})}
\ge
Q^{-A}
\|q''\|_{L^2(W_{\ref})}.
\]

This is likely true, but it does not contradict anything. It just shows trapped solutions are observable.

Thus Gate–1 remains compatible with the ODE trap.

---

## 7. What would exclude the trap?

There are three plausible exclusion mechanisms.

### Mechanism A: actual EF anti-ODE theorem

Prove the canonical explicit-formula source cannot satisfy

\[
\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)\in\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

on a polynomial-weight zero-routed set.

This is an arithmetic non-lock theorem analogous to Gate–1 finite AFE anti-lock, but at fourth order.

### Mechanism B: higher residual normality

If the order-nine \(Y12\) residual is absorbed by the wall ODE, move to the derivative residual

\[
\partial_m\mathcal W_\Gamma
\]

or an order-eleven analogue. Then test whether the new residual has a primitive signature not in the ODE model tangent.

This is a targeted high-order D4 route.

### Mechanism C: rank-two rescue

If another D4 carrier survives independently, the full vector

\[
(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2})
\]

may have wall mass even when

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G=0.
\]

This requires rank two in the forbidden quotient or another rank-one channel with a different wall line.

Thus rank-one \(Y12\) is enough only if the moving wall finite-type theorem holds. If the trap is real, we need additional normal directions.

---

## 8. Updated wall theorem should include trap alternative

The wall theorem should not claim unconditional anti-concentration. It should be stated as:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{MovingWallFiniteTypeOrTrap.}
}
\]

Under low-order \(\rho_\Gamma\), either:

### Wall-good branch

\[
\int
\min(Q^A|H\alpha_9(\Gamma_{G,2}+\rho_\Gamma\Gamma_G)|^2,1)
\,d\nu
\ge Q^{-B}\nu(\mathcal C),
\]

or

### Trap branch

\[
q
\]

is concentrated near the finite ODE model

\[
\mathcal W_\Gamma(q)\in\mathcal C_Y^{\mathrm{arith}}
\]

on a polynomial-weight subset.

The proof must then either:

1. exclude the trap branch, or  
2. route it to a higher-order residual / alternative contradiction.

This is the honest state.

---

## 9. Strategic implication

The \(Y12\) route gives a strong rank-one candidate, but the proof still needs a **wall-trap disposal theorem**.

The current finite-D4 chain is:

\[
\boxed{
Y12\text{ separation}
\Rightarrow
\text{rank-one wall line}
\Rightarrow
\text{finite type or ODE trap}.
}
\]

The unresolved question is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the ODE-trap branch be excluded or converted into another contradiction?}
}
\]

This is now the next downstream bottleneck after proving \(Y12\) survives.

---

## Final result of this attack

The moving wall does not give unconditional mass by itself. It gives a dichotomy:

\[
\boxed{
\text{wall anti-concentration}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\text{low-order fourth-order ODE trap.}
}
\]

The trap is meaningful only if

\[
\rho_\Gamma
\]

is genuinely low-order. If the trap persists, finite rank-one \(Y12\) D4 is not enough; the proof must either show actual EF sources cannot live in that ODE model, or introduce a higher/rank-two residual to escape the wall line.
